THE HOUSES  OF THE DEAD



Your Vulcanverse Adventure Starts Here

Vulcanverse is an open-world solo role-playing game. You can play the books in any order, starting anywhere you like, moving to other books whenever you travel to the region they cover. Instead of a single storyline, there are virtually unlimited adventures.

To play all you need is two dice, an eraser and a pencil. You only need one book to start, but by collecting other books in the series you can explore more of the Vulcanverse.

If you have already adventured using other books in the series, you will know your entry point into this book. Turn to that section now.

If this is your first Vulcanverse book, read the rest of the rules before starting at section [**1**](#_1). You will keep the same adventuring persona throughout the books - starting out as a novice but gradually gaining in power, wealth and experience throughout the series.

**ADVENTURE SHEET**

The Adventure Sheet lists everything you'll need to keep track of while playing. Don't fill it in yet. That will happen as you begin your adventure.

**Attributes**

You have four attributes which typically range from -1 to +2 as you're starting out. You will discover your attribute scores as you play. They are:

Charm: your understanding of people and their motives.

Grace: how agile, supple and quick you are.

Ingenuity: cunning and reasoning, and your ability to think on your feet.

Strength: physical might and endurance.

The maximum possible innate score in an attribute is +5. If you are already at maximum and are told to add to your score, it has no effect.

**Items that augment attributes**

There are items you can acquire that boost your attributes while you have them. These are:

Charm: **laurel wreath** (+1) and **golden lyre** (+2)

Grace: **recurve bow** (+1) and **winged sandals** (+2)

Ingenuity: **hornbook** (+1) and **abacus** (+2)

Strength: **hardwood club** (+1) and **iron spear** (+2)

You can only use the bonus from one such item at a time. So if you had a **laurel wreath** that gives Charm +1 and a **golden lyre** that gives Charm +2, you'd only get the Charm bonus from the latter. Similarly, two **laurel wreaths** still only give you a +1.

An item can augment your attribute score above the innate limit of +5. If you have a Strength score of +5 and you possess an **iron spear**, your total Strength bonus when making a roll counts as +7.

**Making an Attribute Roll**

Attribute rolls are made to see if you succeed at a task. These are rolls of two dice with a difficulty that you must equal or beat to succeed.

For instance, you might be told: 'Make a Strength roll at difficulty 7'. You roll two dice, add your Strength score (including the modifiers for any one possession that boosts Strength) and to succeed you need to get 7 or more.

_Example: You are at the bottom of a cliff. To climb it you need to make a Grace roll at difficulty 5. You roll two dice and score 4. Your Grace attribute is -1 but luckily you have_ **_winged sandals_** _which give a +2 Grace bonus, so your modified Grace is +1, which is just enough to make the roll a success._

A roll of double 6 ('boxcars') is always a success regardless of difficulty. A roll of double 1 ('snake eyes') is always a failure regardless of modifiers.

**WOUNDS**

The Adventure Sheet has a box labelled Wound. This is unticked at the start of the adventure. From time to time you may be asked to put a tick in it. You only have one Wound tick at a time; if you're asked to tick the box when it is already ticked, you don't add another.

While the Wound box is ticked you have injuries, and these will cause you to deduct 1 from any attribute roll until the box is unticked.

**SCARS**

You begin with no scars, but may acquire them from lasting injuries or from returning from the afterlife. Scars are a mixed blessing. Many people will shun you because of them, but others will admire or fear you more.

**POSSESSIONS**

Possessions are always marked in bold text, like this: **iron spear**. If you come across an item marked like this you can pick it up and add it to your list of possessions.

You can carry up to twenty possessions at a time. If you come across an item you want when already at your limit, you'll have to discard something to make room. There are places in the Vulcanverse where you can leave possessions and come back for them later.

**MONEY**

You can carry any sum of money (measured in a currency called pyr). You'll discover as you play whether you have any money to start off with.

**GLORY**

Glory starts at 0 but will grow as you perform deeds that increase your renown. With high Glory you will be recognized as a hero and given more respect by those you meet.

**CODEWORDS**

There is a list of codewords. Sometimes you will be told you have acquired a codeword. When this happens, put a tick in the box next to that codeword. If you later lose the codeword, erase the tick.

The codewords are arranged alphabetically for each book in the series. In this book, for example, all codewords begin with N. This makes it easy to check if you picked up a codeword from a book you played previously. For instance, you might be asked if you have picked up a codeword in a book you have already adventured in. The letter of that codeword will tell you which book to check (eg if it begins with O, it is from Book 2: _The Hammer of the Sun_).

**TITLE**S

Titles record the achievements you have earned, marking you as the champion of a city, protector of a temple, admiral of a fleet, or even a monarch. You will be told when you acquire a title.

**BLESSINGS**

If you fail an attribute roll, you can use up a blessing to roll the dice again. You can only do that once per roll, so you cannot use a second blessing to get another reroll if the first one fails.

You can have up to three blessings at a time. You start the adventure with no blessings. Usually the place to get blessings is at a shrine or temple, but you may find other opportunities to acquire them.

**COMPANION**

As you travel the Vulcanverse, you will meet some people who are willing to journey with you. You can have one companion at a time. When you pick up a new companion you must remove your current companion, if any, from the Companion box. You can also part company with a companion at any time just by deleting their name from the box. You do not have a companion at the start of the adventure.

**CURRENT LOCATION**

You'll use the Current Location box from time to time to keep track of where you are. You will be told when to use it. Whenever you are told to record an entry number in your Current Location box, first delete any number that was already there.

**NOTES**

Use the Notes box on your Adventure Sheet to keep a record of quests, clues or other things you need to remember.

That's all you need to know to get started on your adventure. Now turn to [**1**](#_1)**.**

#### 1

In your waking moments you rarely think of your early life. The world is harsh, drawing a stark line between the quick and the dead, and so there is little time for idle reverie.

But when you sleep and your soul becomes unpinned from your body, time is a river that can flow both ways. Sometimes you see yourself then as an infant, standing on the hard-packed floor of your home as various uncles and aunts come with gifts for your birthday.

There is a man whose face is twisted by a scar that runs across his right eye, a sightless milky orb. He holds out a hand from which an old sword-cut sheared several fingers. Your uncle Nicomachus.

'No, little one, come to me,' calls your aunt Terpe from across the room, singing a little song that makes you laugh in delight.

Your uncle Sophos has a gift for you. 'See these pictures of distant lands,' he says, holding up a book whose coloured pictures blaze as if with inner light. 'When you are a little older you will read the words that describe these lands and travel there in your imagination.'

In the doorway waits your aunt Eremia. She never joins the throng at the hearth, always has one foot outside. 'Imagination's a fine thing. Better still to travel there in person.'

It is as though this moment has happened already. The four wait, and then you go to one of them. But which?

Scary uncle Nicomachus goto [**393**](#_393)

Friendly aunt Terpe goto [**30**](#_30)

Clever uncle Sophos goto [**667**](#_667)

Restless aunt Eremia goto [**114**](#_114)

#### 3

The Acheron river, the river of woe, runs all the way up to its headwaters in the far northern mountains of Hades. It flows slowly into the delta, its waters the colour of twilight, a kind of bluish grey, infused with a strange, starry glow, as if moonbeams were trapped forever on the riverbed.

You see a barge bobbing languidly on its surface. A shadowy figure, long punting pole in hand, waves at you.

'Want to go upriver, sir? Only five pyr?' he shouts. Or rather, whispers loudly for he too, like nearly all of the denizens of the underworld, is a shade. You notice the barge is called _Shadow on the Water_. It is black, as it seems are all the barges of Hades, but this one is lined with purple trim. A flag flies from the stern - a black kraken on a purple background. Will you:

Hire the barge goto [**765**](#_765)

Go south-west to woodlands goto [**228**](#_228)

Go north-west to the Black Bayou goto [**273**](#_273)

East overland goto [**51**](#_51)

#### 4

You approach the sculpted portico of the mausoleum. It is pale yellow in colour, and somehow untouched by age, as if it were newly erected. There is a low entrance at the bottom of the portico, with an inscription nearby. It reads:

'a maid of gold am i. i mark the grave of midas. while the black waters flow and the white trees grow tall, here will i watch over the tear-drenched tomb and tell them that my father lies here.'

So, it appears this is the tomb of the legendary King Midas. The entrance appears to be a simple open doorway, seemingly unguarded.

Enter the tomb of Midas goto [**221**](#_221)

Leave this area goto [**76**](#_76)

#### 6

Prometheus leans down towards you. 'I have nothing to reward you with now, as you can imagine after all this time getting my liver eaten, but the day will come when I will answer your call and stand by your side, human, that I swear to you. For now, I give you this title - the Liberator!'

Gain the title **the Liberator**. Now that Prometheus is free, all the great iron doors and their mouthy guardians have fallen open and the three of you make your way back to the hole that you and Deucalion floated down. Prometheus, giant-sized as he is, easily climbs up and out, from where he lowers down some ropes to haul you both out.

Deucalion says his farewells, thanking you.

'Perhaps we will meet again,' he says before Prometheus picks him up and turns to leave.

'There will be a time when you will need me, mortal, and I will be there, I swear it!' he booms as he strides across the landscape. Soon you are alone once more.

goto [**234**](#_234)

#### 7

You hold up the sceptre and say, 'I claim this sceptre as my own. It is mine to wield!'

'No...' wails Agamemnon. He staggers, as does his son. The darkness that filled them with energy flows out of them to plunge into the sceptre. Then that darkness, like creeping twilight, seeps from the sceptre to shroud you in a shadowy nimbus of night.

Agamemnon and his son seem to shrivel up in front of you, becoming ancient shades of the dead once more. They stare at you, a look of empty, forgotten despair on their phantasmal faces.

'What now, father?' mutters Orestes.

'I am a father? I can't recall. What was I? Did we..? Ah, so much is lost, so much...'

They turn and shuffle away, sad lonely figures. You follow. Outside, all of his followers, his army of the dead, have also fallen into emptiness, reverting to ancient shades wandering the gloomy halls of Hades, no longer animated by the power of night.

Agamemnon's rule is over. Inside yourself, you can feel tiny tendrils of darkness worming their way into your heart. Now it is you who hear the call of the night. What have you done?

goto [**700**](#_700)

#### 8

Leave the slopes goto [**410**](#_410)

Put a tick in the box. If there was a tick in the box already goto [**816**](#_816) immediately. If not, read on.

'Ah, welcome back to my workshop,' says Perdix the inventor, murdered nephew of Daedalus.

Perdix, inventor, craftsman, and smelter can perform various tasks for you. If you have some **copper ore**, you can get him to make you a **copper cog** on a one for one basis. For each **copper ore** you cross off, you can get one **copper cog**. This is free of charge, as he doesn't use all the ore to make a cog. You can also buy a few things from him.

			To buy		To sell
clockwork owl	        50 pyr	 	 –
brazen trumpet	        50 pyr	  	–
vinegar of Hades	20 pyr		15 pyr

When you are done, Perdix asks you a question. 'Will you help me? I have made this.'

He shows you a beautifully engraved copper casket.

'What does it do?' you ask.

'I won't lie to you. It is carved with a puzzle that only Daedalus himself could solve. When it is opened, it will explode and hopefully kill my uncle outright. Will you take it? Give it to him if you see him. My revenge will then be complete.'

'If I take this, how do you know I'll give it to him at all?'

'I don't. But my guess is, knowing my uncle, that once you've met him, you'll hate him too. Or perhaps not, but worth a shot, I figure.'

Note the **copper casket of doom** on your Adventure Sheet, should you decide to take it.

When you are ready, if you have the title **Lion Slayer** and do _not_ possess the **Nemean** **bagh nakh** goto [**790**](#_790)

#### 10

The city is divided into roughly four quarters or suburbs - the Palace, atop the hill, mostly empty now; Elysium, where the souls of the honoured dead reside; the Asphodel Meadows, where the souls of those who were neither famous nor notorious or those simply unworthy of Elysium reside; and the Mourning Fields for those who cannot break the bonds of tragedy from their former lives, bonds that bind them in grief and sorrow or broken-hearted, unrequited love. The pits of Tartarus (for those who truly offended the gods) are elsewhere.

Nearby, you notice an enterprising merchant has set up a shop of some kind. Where will you go:

#### 13

Ignoring her call, you swim out into the cool, black waters. It is actually quite pleasant on the skin, like swimming in some kind of moisturiser, albeit black. It doesn't even smell that bad. You begin to relax - until something slithers around your waist. And then something else around your throat. Tentacles? A huge snake?

There is no time to find out, for you are dragged down into the depths, into utter darkness where you cannot even see your hand in front of your face. You are crushed, drowned and devoured in the inky darkness of the bayou. Fortunately, the gods have not abandoned you, despite your stupidity.

goto [**111**](#_111)

#### 14

You hurl the **shrivelled heart** to one side of the chamber, hoping the maggots will go for it. But to them it is just another piece of rotting flesh, of which they have a surfeit. On the other hand, you are fresh and still living, and the act of throwing the heart has alerted them to your presence.

They swarm all over you, a writhing mass of mouths, eager to feed upon you. You are overwhelmed and devoured in seconds. At least death is quick. And for you, it is not the end. goto [**111**](#_111)

The Garden of Persephone is a desert of black ash, a charred, smouldering ruin. Smoke still twirls up from the burned out pavilion.

Suddenly you are overwhelmed by fatigue. A strange sleepiness steals over your limbs and you sink into an unexpected slumber. In your dreams, Persephone herself comes to you, dressed in long flowing robes, her skin pale like alabaster. She wears a wreath of flowers around her head, and she is breathtakingly beautiful, even when angry, as she is now, with a face like thunder.

'How dare you burn my garden up like that, you barbarian! Brute! Thug! Ruffian!'

This is not good, you are being berated by a goddess - you beg for forgiveness. If you have the codewords **_Oedipus_** or **_Olifant_** or the title **Favoured of Demeter** goto [**616**](#_616) immediately. If not, read on.

#### 19

You take the arrow. 'Thank you,' you say, 'but are you seriously telling me that if I shoot this arrow, it will never reach its target?'

'Try it,' says Xenophanes, 'Let us test the hypothesis!'

'This should be interesting,' says Cleanthes, raising his eyes.

You nock the arrow to a bow and aim at a nearby tree. The philosophers watch, fascinated. All except Aristippus who is takes the opportunity to steal food from everyone's plates while his colleagues are distracted. You let fly.

#### 21

There is an old half-rotted rope that runs down into the depths. It seems solid enough, so you inch your way down. Slowly the light dims as you descend. At the bottom, the well is dry but you find some grisly human remains. It appears to be the long dead body of a girl. Judging by the angle of her head, she fell - or perhaps was thrown - down the well and broke her neck. Thin, parchment-like skin is drawn tautly over the small skull. Her tattered clothes seem to have once been a pale blue in colour but now they are ragged. But wait - is that a dried bloodstain around what could be a stab wound? Who - or what - would stab a child and then throw them down a well?

You notice tiny silver buckles on her pitted leather sandals, shaped like little flowers, but then a doleful sob catches your attention. You look to the side and see an alcove set back from the well bottom and, within it, eyes glowing palely in the dark.

Get out of here goto [**168**](#_168)

Wait and see what happens goto [**516**](#_516)

#### 23

Lose the codewords **_Neverending_** and **_Projectile_**. Gain the codeword **_Neveragain_**.

You let loose at Wolfshadow as he runs in on you. The arrow hits home unerringly, right between his eyes. With one final whimper, the undead wolf collapses in a heap and slides up to your feet, dead in an instant. You can hardly believe it. Cross the **golden arrow of Artemis** off your Adventure Sheet; its purpose done, it has dissipated into nothingness.

After you've burned the hideous, flayed body of the undead wolf, just to be sure, you investigate the cave.

It is a grisly sight. There is blood everywhere with numerous dismembered bodies scattered around, gnawed bones, half eaten heads - a den of utter horror. But you are a hardened adventurer, nothing stands between you and your loot! You find amidst the ruined, twisted flesh of his victims, 150 pyr, a **lava gem**, some **honey of Hades** (made in the gardens of Persephone, removes one wound; cross it off after use) and the **Helm of the Lost Hero**. This ancient helmet is rusted and cracked, and is of no use on its own, but perhaps it will be useful later. Better hold on to it...

For your heroic deed, you also get +1 Glory.

Now goto [**367**](#_367)

#### 26

If you have some **shade warriors** goto [**126**](#_126) immediately. If not, read on.

Two pale and ghostly figures step out to block your path. They are dressed as hoplite warriors armed with spear and shield but their antique armour is tarnished and dented, their weapons rusty. They are lost souls who died in battle that still wander the mournful roads of Hades, clinging to half-forgotten memories of glory, even in death.

'Glory to the victors!' says one in a deep, sepulchral voice.

'Blood for our lord Ares!' says the other.

They cannot be reasoned with for now they know only one thing - battle lust. You will have to fight these shade warriors.

#### 27

'I have your **gold obols** here, dread lord,' you say. Cross them off your Adventure Sheet.

'Thank you, mortal, that is a relief. She was an impudent thief!'

'A minx indeed,' you reply.

'Caused me all sorts of problems, admin wise. You have no idea what it's like having to keep track of the all the souls, who's paid and who hasn't, who goes to Elysium or Tartarus and all that!'

'I can imagine,' you sympathise, as you wait for your reward.

Charon shrugs. At least it looks like a shrug, it's hard to tell under all those tattered black shrouds and the heavy black cowl. Charon waits. You wait. And then:

'Oh, I see! Of course, you deserve a reward. Let me see, what have we here?'

Charon rummages through various items that have ended up in his barge.

'Here we are, this seems to be the sort of thing you warrior heroes like,' and he hands you a suit of **sepulchral armour**. It is an ancient rusting suit of ceremonial armour from a bygone age, really to be worn at funerals rather than in a real fight or battle, but still, you can hardly refuse Charon himself.

He nods at you, so you don the armour. Note **sepulchral armour x 1** on your Adventure Sheet. It will block one wound before it falls to pieces (cross it off after). You also gain the title **Friend of the Ferryman**. Note it on your Adventure Sheet too.

'Better than nothing, I suppose,' you mutter as you take your leave.

From here you can go:

#### 28

If you have a **baby in a basket** stored here or on your Adventure Sheet goto [**300**](#_300). If not, read on.

You are in King Agamemnon's Isle of the Blessed. He has gifted you a rather fine villa in Elysium. It is a lovely spot in the only place in Hades where the sun shines, even if it is a fake, magical sun. You have a few vases filled with flowers of Elysium. Although black and veined in red, they look quite fetching and the air is filled with a not unpleasant scent, a welcome relief from the smell of decay and death that pervades elsewhere. You even have a servant, supposedly a faithful, ghostly shade named Briseis, although it seems she doesn't quite see it that way. She calls herself the rightful inhabitant of the villa and often rails at you to leave and that you don't belong here and nor does King Agamemnon.

But anyway, here you can relax (well, sometimes), rest and heal yourself if you are wounded (untick your Wound box, if ticked). You can also store items or money or retrieve them, as you wish. Simply write them in the box or add them to your sheet. Don't forget to cross them off your Adventure Sheet or Store when depositing/making a withdrawal.

#### 29

Something seems off to you. A little pushback might be in order. 'And what if I refuse to pay?' you say.

'Then your soul will wander these shores for a hundred years.'

'But I am not dead,' you reply.

'Bah, You would defy me, the great Charon, son of Nyx? I'll have you chained in the belly of Tartarus where your liver will be torn out and eaten every day by harpies!' he bellows, his voice now sounding rather raucous and shrill. Such a fate would be awful indeed - if he is who he says he is. What will you do?

#### 30

You can have a total of three blessings at a time. Each blessing can be used to roll again when you fail an attribute check (Charm, Grace, Ingenuity or Strength) but only once in any given situation - so if you fail the re-roll, you can't spend another blessing to try again.

#### 38

The Slimeswamp is a fetid morass of steaming muck and foul, brackish water. Small strips of impacted mud form pathways through the quagmire. Corpses lie just below the surface, somehow preserved despite the filthy waters. Mangrave trees grow out of the slime - half corpse, half tree, twisted faces leering at you out of gnarled tree trunks. Their branches stretch out, and on the end of them foul egg-like seeds are ready to drop onto the drowned corpses. You can see one corpse that has such a seed already germinating within it. You wonder how the swamp keeps up its supply of corpses when one of the trees' boughs twitches and reaches for you, branches grasping like fingers. Luckily they are quite slow and you are able to jump back. As you do so, you put your foot on one of the submerged corpses' chests - its eyes flick open for a moment, before the eyelids close up again. You approach the rotting hulk of a once mighty trireme, prow down in the mire of the delta. Though its timbers are encrusted with mud and decay, you can still see its sleek lines and the graceful cut of its bow. The figurehead is strangely preserved, though; it is of white-armed Hera, queen of the gods.

As you draw near, you notice the remains of a man, crushed under the mouldering prow of the boat. Hera seems to be gazing down at the body.

Wiping some black filth away, you are able to make out the name of the ship. It is the _Argo_. Curiosity piqued, you climb up the sides of the ship to explore.

goto [**116**](#_116)

#### 40

You enter a large, rough cavern of solid rock, so large its roof is shrouded in shadow. It is lit by the glow of a river of lava that runs along one side, warming up the cave nicely. In the middle is an enormous rock upon which is pinned the body of a giant - the titan Prometheus. His arms and legs are held in place by massive chains of bronze. There is a lot of scarring along one side of his body where, as a cruel punishment for his crimes, a giant eagle rips out his liver every day and eats it by order of the great Zeus. Prometheus' liver then regenerates overnight, ready for another day of torture, lying there, alone, abandoned, waiting for the eagle to come down and feed. You cannot imagine his suffering. How cruel the gods can be! And his crime? Creating man from clay and then compounding that by teaching them about fire, and the arts of civilisation.

As you draw near he turns his massive head. His face is covered in an enormous beard but his eyes show a lifetime of suffering, and his brow seems furrowed in a crease of permanent pain. He stares at you both in astonishment.

'Can it be... is that you, my son?' he says in an ancient rasping voice.

'Yes father, it is I, Deucalion!'

'Did your mother..?'

'Yes, I was rebirthed, but she was forced to give me up to the river or lose me forever. Luckily for us, this adventurer found me, and brought me up.'

Suddenly there is an ear-splitting shriek and out of the shadows up above a great bird comes hurtling down.

'The Caucasian eagle, spawn of Typhon!' shouts Prometheus, his face a mask of terror. 'No, no...' he mutters, straining desperately against his bronze chains.

'Quickly, break the chains, whilst I distract the eagle!' cries Deucalion. He backs away and starts laying out various raw and bloody meats from his backpack onto the ground. The eagle, having eaten only the liver of Prometheus for only the gods know how long, rushes to eat up the delicious treats. It lands with a great squawk, and starts feeding. But how much meat was Deucalion able to stuff in his backpack?

You have to break the chains that bind the titan quickly. Will you use:

Raw power goto [**218**](#_218)

Your brains goto [**84**](#_84)

#### 61

Lachesis, the one in white, holds out her hand. The other two Fates' brows furrow in puzzlement.

'Now, give me that honey cake,' says Lachesis, in a remarkably normal voice, 'the one you're supposed to give me.'

'Cake? When the fate of this world hangs in the hands of this hero?' says Atropos.

'Yes, honey cake! What's the point of controlling destiny itself if you can't give yourself a little treat from time to time,' says Lachesis grumpily. She glares at you. 'Now give me that honey cake. After all, it is written, so it shall be, destiny is all, the fates have spoken and so on.'

You find yourself unable to refuse, and you hand over the **honey cake**. Cross it off your Adventure Sheet.

'Thank you,' says Lachesis, grabbing the cake and stuffing it into her mouth entire.

'You didn't even share a singly crumb,' says Clotho in a disgusted voice.

'Well, what can I say?' mumbles Lachesis with her mouth full. 'It was written in the stars, so what could I do?'

'Anyway, thank you, mortal,' she says. 'Here, have this.' And she turns her attention to the skein of destiny on the podium, the thread that is your life. She makes an adjustment.

Note that you have the **Favour of the Fates x 1** on your Adventure Sheet. The next attribute roll you fail will automatically be reversed and succeed instead. If and when that happens, cross off the **Favour**. (Effectively it is a one-use blessing that will always succeed.)

'What, you interfere with the skein of destiny - for cake?'

'Yup!' says, Lachesis.

Clotho heaves a great sigh, shaking her head. She turns her attention back to you.

'Now, mortal,' says Clotho, 'choose your destiny.'

'It's not really a choice though, is it?' says Lachesis.

'Indeed, we already know what it's going to be,' says Atropos.

The inhabitants do not care about their surroundings, no offerings are left at the shrines, the walkways are pitted and overgrown with the thorny, black brambles of Hades, or overshadowed by the skeletal branches of bone-white poplar trees. Everywhere there is an all-pervading sense of sorrow and despair, and wherever you go, you cannot escape the sound of sobbing or the keening wails of grief and misery that pierce the gloom like needles of anguish. This is an awful place.

The Asphodel Meadows are a part of Hades reserved for the shades of ordinary folk, neither heroes nor villains. It is indeed full of meadows, covered in asphodels, but these asphodels are blooms of the underworld - pallid and pale, ghostly flowers of the afterlife. Elysium, also known as the Isle of the Blessed, is separated from the necropolis by a river - of a kind. It is not a river of water, but an abyssal gulf filled with distant celestial bodies, strange constellations and fiery comet trails - the River of Stars. Elysium is in a dimension of its own. A bridge spans the River of Stars, leading to a verdant isle, not as green as Arcadia, perhaps, but certainly a place of light and warmth compared to the rest of the underworld. Only those who were heroic, honourable, righteous and just in life may cross the bridge. And also the living, presumably, as none of them are supposed to ever be in Hades in the first place.

The bridge, made of sleek ebony inlaid with a kind of parquet patterning of white wood from the underworld poplar, is beautiful to look at, but as you draw near, you notice something odd. The bridge has been blocked off with a barricade of shields and outwardly pointing javelins. Behind them stand several ghostly forms, dressed in antique armour from the Trojan wars, a troop of ancient warriors.

'I am the captain of the bridge, and you cannot pass,' one of them shouts. 'Elysium is now ruled by King Agamemnon, and only he decides who can enter or remain.'

You notice that these shades seem a little more corporeal than most, and their voices have a stronger tone to them, more vigorous than the reedy voices of the spectral shades you are used to. There are several of them, well-armed veterans by the look of them.

The throne room of Hades is a long hall hewn out of living rock. Its walls are rugged grey granite, but lined with black marble pillars inlaid with silver filigree. Black and silver drapes hang on the walls; the ceiling is painted with stars, as if you were looking up at a midnight sky; the floor is a black and white mosaic depicting various underworld scenes - Cerberus guarding the gates, Charon ferrying souls across the Styx, a tribunal judging the dead, the Furies punishing the guilty, other shades taking their ease in the paradise of Elysium and so on. Jewel-encrusted iron lanterns hang from the ceiling, filling the place with a pale brightness.

At one end rests a double throne made of shining ebony but there is no one sitting upon it. The hall is deserted, for all the Olympian gods save Vulcan are sleeping. Not even a lowly shade is to be seen. It is as quiet as... well, the grave. At the far end, double doors lead out of the throne room. Behind the throne, you see a strange-looking plinth, like a sundial.

Your soul recognizes him immediately - it is Thanatos, he whose touch is death, and whose heart is iron.

Beside him stands his brother, Hypnos, he whose breath is sleep and whose words are dreams. He too is winged, and bearded, with a wreath of bright red poppies on his head, but his robes and beard are white and inviting, like bedsheets. His eyes are like pale portals into a world of dreams.

#### 110

You lead them to that forgotten corner of the underworld where there is an ancient graveyard, a long abandoned temple to Hades and, behind that, the stables where the winged chariot is stored. The chariot's wings spread wide, the bulls, snorting fire and steam, begin a hammering gallop, and then the chariot soars skyward. You sweep over Hades below.

Lampeto screams in delight - as far as someone who has been dead for longer than you've been alive can scream in delight that is. After a while, you descend into the mountains of Boreas.

'Thank you, my saviour,' says Lampeto, resting a ghostly hand on your shoulder. 'As I am - or at least once was - a Queen of the Amazons, I dub thee my champion!'

Note the title **Queen's Champion** on your Adventure Sheet.

Lampeto says her farewells. 'We will find a place here, but then you must come back to me. There is more work we can do together. The more you aid me, the greater will your final reward, trust me!' With that, the three shades disappear into mountains and are gone. From here, you can either:

Alight in Boreas **? 222** in [_The Pillars of the Sky_](https://amzn.to/4kterJS)

#### 136

Hydna punts the _Threnody_ down a small waterway through a swampy morass of twisted foliage until it opens out in another hidden lake, in the middle of which are three hills. The first has a man heaving and sweating his way up one side whilst rolling a heavy boulder up to the top.

'These are some of the terrible punishments the gods exact for our temerity,' says Hydna. 'There is Sisyphus, forever cursed. When he finally gets that rock up to the top, it will fall down again, and once more must he strain and struggle to lift it up. He's been doing that for an age.'

'What was his crime?'

'He cheated death for a while, the lot of all mortals, but even worse in the eyes of the gods, he claimed to be more cunning and intelligent than Zeus. The gods hate hubris above all things.'

The next hill has a burning wheel at the top, slowly turning. On it a man is tied, never dying, never consumed by the flames just suffering the torments of fire. Forever.

'What an awful fate - what was his crime?' you ask.

'That is Ixion. He burned his father in law to death. But that wasn't his greatest crime. He tried to ravish Hera, the queen of the gods. He managed to both offend the gods as a kin-slayer and then anger white-armed Hera and her husband, Zeus the Thunderer.'

'Why would you risk the wrath of the gods like that?' you ask with a shake of the head.

'Some say he was mad.'

The last hill has a man chained to the top. On either side, great bronze gongs ring out, giving off a deafening, thundering sound. 'That is Salmoneus,' says Hydna, 'once a king, and brother to Sisyphus. He made a bridge of bronze, across which he drove a chariot, thus making a booming, thunderous sound. And so he claimed that he was Zeus the Thunderer and should be worshipped and that the other was an impostor who did not even exist.'

'Seems legit...'

'As you can imagine, Zeus struck him with a thunderbolt thus proving that he did indeed exist, and then chained him down here, to suffer eternal cacophonous thunder. Every night his ear drums regenerate.'

'The gods are cruel,' you say.

'They are indeed,' replies Hydna. 'And that concludes our tour of cruel and unusual punishments!'

'Well, thank you, another fascinating tour,' you say. Will you:

It is actually quite refreshing as the winds of Hades are usually redolent with death, decay and despair. To the north, the way ends. You cannot get into the mountains of Boreas from here for the veil of the shadow of death that surrounds the underworld prevents all travel into and out of Hades, save through its two great gates. However, there is also a range of northern mountains that are within the realm of the dead.

#### 149

One of the Furies notices you. She flies down, hissing and spitting with angry bile, to alight nearby.

'Sisters!' she screams. 'It is the kin-slayer, who slew their own brother!'

Another comes down to alight beside her.

'Helpful of them to come to us, saving us a trip.'

You prepare your weapon as the third arrives - is it even possible to fight the Furies? Perhaps you are about to find out, but then the third says:

'Wait, this one is still alive!'

'Alive, here in our own house?' says the second.

'We cannot touch them, even here,' says the third.

'Hades wither your heart, mortal,' says the first, 'Hurry up and die, so we can mete out the punishment you deserve.'

With that the Furies fly back to their business with a curse and a threat or two, but that is all.

There is little you can do here. You can hardly free everyone, nor should you perhaps, without knowing their crimes and it would not be prudent to take on the Furies, no matter how heroic you are. #### 151

You step into the chamber of the Fates, the Moirai. And there they are, all three of them, before you. Immediately in your heart you seem to know who and what they are - Lachesis sings the things that were, Clotho the things that are, and Atropos the things that will be.

One of the Fates sits at a chair, weaving the threads of destiny on her spindle, she is Clotho the Spinner. As she weaves, she hands threads to Lachesis, Disposer of Lots, who is dressed in white and measures each thread on a podium. Thus is a mortal's destiny decided.

And then there is the third Fate, sitting at a low table - Atropos the Inexorable, who wields a pair of shears and cuts the thread of life. They speak in low, portentous voices, laden with doom.

'You are the hero of this age and therefore we cannot -' says Clotho.

'- be sure of your destiny, unlike lesser mortals, though we know it will be one of three - ' continues Lachesis.

' - possible outcomes, and you must choose wisely but at the end it will all come down to the roll of the cosmic dice,' finishes Atropos, 'and even we cannot know that score.'

Clotho points to the other end of the chamber, where another stairwell leads down into the depths.

'That leads to Erebus, where the titan, night-cowled Nyx and her sons, Thanatos the undoer and ever-dreaming Hypnos reside,' says white-robed Lachesis.

'Do not enter there if you are not ready,' says Atropos.

#### 158

#### 159

The spiders inject their venom into you. You can feel it burning its way up your body, paralysing your limbs. You fall to the ground, unable to move. Fear and panic fill your heart as they bundle you up and submerge you in the vile waters of the swamp near a tree. You stare up through the murky waters as thin mangrave roots squirm into your body from below, feeding you with oxygen. Some days pass in paralytic horror until finally you see a swollen seed-egg over your head. Another day later, and the seed egg bursts open and a long, thin tendril slowly worms its way down to burrow into your mouth and down your throat.

You come round, days later, your body twisted and melded into that of the mangrave tree you have now become. Memories of your previous life slowly fade as you are filled with a kind of yearning rage, to feed upon mankind and to spread your seed. Not even the gods can save you from this fate, for you are not dead, merely... different. Your adventure ends here.

#### 160

The ghostly bard's voice is thin and reedy, but it reverberates through the air, like a spectral echo. He plays his lyre and sings of the great battle between Typhon, king of the monsters and Zeus, king of the gods. They fought for days, Typhon hurling mountains and fireballs from his eyes, Zeus throwing thunderbolt after thunderbolt at his foe. Every time Zeus hit his foe, bits of Typhon were blown off, to fall all over the earth. Except that Typhon was said to be so big his head touched the stars, so he was able to endure such pain. Until one of Zeus's bolts took out an eyeball. It fell into the desert, where it hit with such force that ripples of sand were blown outward from its landing place, like a rock hurled into a pond. Thus were created the Dunes of Doom. An interesting tale.

#### 181

'Well,' says Protagoras, 'although we have not definitively proved Zeno's arrow paradox, we have not disproved it either.'

'Bah, more sophistry,' says Cleanthes.

'According to your methods, it can never be proved, as you will always reword the evidence to suit your arguments,' says Xenophanes.

'Well, our adventurer has the arrow, perhaps they will go forth and prove the paradox one way or the other for us. Please return if you do,' says Aspasia. 'In the meantime, I have another question for you all to consider.'

'Good! Anything that will stop us wasting time with Protagoras' ludicrous word games.' says Cleanthes.

'Ludicrous word games? How dare you. After all, isn't that the basis of all philosophy, and thus the basis of all art, science and religion - word games.'

'Nonsense, there is also hypothesis, experiment, evidence and conclusion,' says Xenophanes.

'Bah, also just words, and all a waste of time, life is here to be endured, not understood,' barks Cleanthes the Stoic.

'Endured? Only in the pool of your own self-made misery, Cleanthes! No, life is here to be enjoyed,' says Aristippus.

Protagoras adds gleefully, 'Ah, but Cleanthes, don't you see? My word games are the very thing that you must endure. Thus, it is I who give your life meaning.'

Cleanthes throws his hands into the air, 'Bah, and double bah! Listening to you is like the suffering of Prometheus - chained to a rock and having his liver pecked out every day by an eagle.'

'Surely a Stoic's dream!' says Protagoras.

'Argh...' wails Cleanthes. Aristippus bursts into laughter. Xenophanes shakes his head.

You are about to leave them to their bickering when Aspasia says, 'Now, now, gentlemen, please. Listen. Just before I... arrived here... I was in Athens, holding a philosophers' symposium. A merchant came and showed me a scroll from the far, far east, way beyond Persia...'

The philosophers fall silent, their curiosity piqued. As is yours, in fact. You decide to wait and hear this tale before leaving.

Davora is dead. You head back to the trapdoor from which she came to investigate her lair.

There is a ladder down into an old tomb, long abandoned but now the lair of the lamia. The stench is unbelievable, it is all you can do stop yourself throwing up. Using a scented cloth you descend.

You find heaps of bones in one corner, many showing the marks of gnawing teeth. A row of skulls on one shelf mark her victims. Each skull has a small name plate beneath, as mementoes. They say things like 'Alexios, boy child, sweet as pie' or 'Melita, actress, bitter all the way through' or 'Leander, fought well, tasted better' and so on. You also find a small pile of papyrus letters, correspondence between her and her sister, another lamia called Mormo.

Amongst the broken remnants of her victims' possessions you find 75 pyr, a still workable **hardwood club** and a **laurel wreath**. Add them to your Adventure Sheet.

Of even more interest is what you find in another corner - a shrine to the king of the monsters, the dragon Typhon, spawn of Gaia and Tartarus. Beside it is a papyrus scroll that explains how to cast a spell of binding that will invoke the power of Typhon.

#### 197

You make a shambling run for it, but the body on your shoulder is too heavy, and several spiders catch up to you. One of them bites you in the ankle, injecting venom into to you. You can feel it burning its way up your body, and then suddenly you freeze and fall to the ground. You are unable to move. To your horror the spiders bundle you up and carry you back to the vile waters of the swamp. There they submerge you near a tree, and you stare up through the murky waters as thin mangrave roots squirm into your body from below, feeding you with oxygen. Some days pass in paralytic horror until finally you see a swollen seed-egg over your head. Another day later, and the seed egg bursts open and a long, thin tendril slowly worms its way down to burrow into your mouth and down your throat.

You come round, days later, your body twisted and melded into that of the mangrave tree you have now become. Memories of your previous life slowly fade as you are filled with a kind of yearning rage, to feed upon mankind and to spread your seed. Not even the gods can save you from this fate, for you are not dead, merely... different. Your adventure ends here.

#### 227

The philosophers stand behind you as you nock the arrow to your bow and take aim at a tree. Other shades from the inn have gathered to watch the experiment, perhaps hoping to see an arrow halted in mid-air.

You draw and let loose. The arrow thuds into a tree and the small crowd heaves a sigh of disappointment which, as they are all spectral shades, sounds like the soughing of the wind through trees. Sacrotes and Plota shrug.

'Well, that was to be expected I suppose, says Plota.

'Ah, except I was aiming for that other tree, behind that one, and I never miss. See, it has indeed been halted half way to its target!' you say, echoing the sophistry of Protagoras...

'But... but that means...' mutters Plota.

'It means it really is Zeno's arrow and the proposition is true, by Athena's arse!' says Sacrotes.

'Well in regard to this particular arrow at least,' says Plota.

'Anyway, will you sign an affidavit to that effect?' you ask.

'What, certifying what we have seen?' says Plota, somewhat warily.

'Yes, indeed, with the signed declaration of the two greatest philosophers it will cause a sensation in the symposiums of the living!' you say.

'Indeed. But what's in it for us?' says Sacrotes.

'The recognition you deserve, of course. You'll be remembered as the philosophers who proved Zeno's paradox to be true.'

They stare at you for a moment and then exchange a look.

'Bring papyrus and quill, immediately!' shouts Sacrotes.

'At last, the recognition we deserve! Now don't forget the ink!' adds Plota.

The entrance to the tomb of Pelops is a large stone door with a marble portico covered in friezes depicting Pelops' accomplishments - winning chariot races, serving Poseidon, founding the Olympic Games, ruling the Peloponnesus (named for him). It is slightly ajar.

'Tantalus?' you say, lightly pushing the door.

It swings open as if on recently oiled hinges and you step inside. Ahead, a large frieze-carved sarcophagus rests in the centre of a circular chamber. The walls are painted with more scenes from the life of Pelops. It is a fine tomb.

Suddenly the door slams shut behind you and the room is plunged into absolute darkness.

'Hah, you fool!' Tantalus crows from the other side of the portal. 'You think I would share the golden dog or anything of mine with a worthless worm like you? No, you will fester and die in there, a suitable companion in death for my burdensome and rebellious son. I, though, am free. Free!'

His last cry fades on the wind as he scampers away. You test the door. It appears to have been barred from the outside.

#### 242

They huddle together and begin to whisper between themselves. After a few minutes, Aspasia say, 'We have decided to impart some of the wisdom of the great philosophers to you as your reward,' she says. She gestures to Cleanthes to start.

'Patience is bitter, but its fruit is sweet - so said Aristotle,' says Cleanthes the Stoic.

'Nothing exists except atoms and empty space; everything else is just opinion - so said Democritus,' says old Xenophanes.

'There is nothing permanent except change - so said Heraclitus,' says Protagoras the Sophist.

'By all means, get married. If you find a good partner, you'll be happy; if not, you'll become a philosopher - so said Socrates himself,' says Aristippus the hedonist.

And finally, Aspasia: 'Every heart sings a song, incomplete, until another heart whispers back. Those who wish to sing always find a song - so said Plato.'

You cross your arms. 'A bag of gold or a weapon made by the gods, or perhaps a clever device of some kind, would have been more useful.'

'Hah! These aphorisms of the great philosophers are worth far more than the mere baubles of earthly existence,' says Protagoras.

'Indeed, think of strengthening your mind rather than your body for once,' says Xenophanes.

'After all, no hero can achieve greatness without a strong mind,' says Cleanthes.

'Bah, they're talking nonsense of course,' says Aristippus, 'their words are indeed largely useless. A nice bag of gold would have been great, but then again, we are philosophers, so what did you expect?'

You shrug. He has a point. If you have a **bronze butterfly** goto [**478**](#_478). If not, read on.

You are about to give up, when the stone door of the trapdoor grinds upward with a screeching creak, and Davora the lamia slithers her way to the surface, no doubt on the hunt for lost souls to feed upon. This time the tables are indeed quite thoroughly turned, for you leap out of your hiding place with a cry of vengeful rage, taking the lamia completely by surprise. With one fell strike, you lop off her head just like that, snicker snack. Her head falls at your feet, her eyes flick up to look at you - she seems to recognize you and tries to mutter one final curse, but it dies on her lips along with the malicious light in her eyes. Her serpentine body thrashes and twitches in its death throes before it too, falls quiescent.

You congratulate yourself on a job well done. That's one less evil monster in the world - no longer will she drink the souls of the dead or the blood of the living.

goto [**187**](#_187)

#### 273

You walk through marshy pathways of withered cypress trees and white poplars that lead to the swampy bayou of Hades until you can walk no further for you have reached the shores of a deep, dark lake.

The underground river, the Lethe, and other tributaries of the Acheron have pooled here, floating up to the surface to form the thin, pitch-black, oily waters called the Black Bayou. Riverside dens and watery graves line its inky waters, punts and flat bottomed funeral barges ply its lakes and waterways, manned by the souls of those drowned at sea.

Hydras are said to live in the bayou, their many heads arguing amongst themselves over the spoils of harvested souls and the fruits of putrefaction and decay. The hydras even have a king, Polykephales, who is said to dwell on an inner island of the bayou.

Nearby, moored to a jetty of ebony, you spot a grand funeral barge, lacquered in shiny black varnish, trimmed with silver. A large catafalque, draped in black velvet, sits amidships. The velvet drape is adorned with the symbol of a poplar tree, picked out in silver thread. A figure on the prow is waving at you. Will you:

#### 274

Suddenly, behind the shade warriors, something huge writhes up out of the water with a screeching roar. It is a massive tentacled creature like an enormous squid the size of a house.

'Aieee, a kraken!' screams one of the Argonauts. You step back, weapon at the ready. The kraken reaches for the three warrior shades with multiple tentacles, picks them up as if they were bread sticks and swallows them down, armour and everything, all in a few seconds.

You think you are next but it seems this creature is immune to the memory erasing powers of the Lethe for it is the very same kraken you once fed, and it remembers you. You marvel at how big it has become!

Thankfully, rather than chomping you down like you were a candy snack, the kraken chirrups a greeting. Perhaps it thought it was saving you from the three warrior shades. The creature reaches into the river, and then gently lays down some things at your feet, including a bottle of **Lethe water x3**. It has three doses of the waters of forgetfulness. Do not drink it yourself! It may prove useful elsewhere, however. The kraken also gifts you an **iron spear** and some **fresh fish**. It then clacks with its beak with what really feels like a goodbye, before sinking below the waves of the Lethe and out of sight.

#### 277

To your astonishment, the Panoply of the Lost Hero rises up and assembles itself into the form of an invisible warrior. All you can see is the helm, the cuirass, the greaves and the vambraces! The suit of armour steps up to the sealed door and with a click the door slides to the side, as if it somehow recognises it. The panoply steps into the ancient tomb, with you following, jaws agape in amazement.

As you enter into the circular chamber, torches on the wall light up as if by magic, revealing a mural running around the walls but you cannot make out the story it is telling, for it is faded and pitted with age.

In the middle is a funereal dais, upon which lie the mummified remains of what was once a tall, powerfully built woman-warrior. She wears a simple tunic, with an old, rusty shield resting against one side of the dais, and a long spear on the other. You are further astonished to see the panoply begin to fit itself around the ancient corpse, the greaves wrapping themselves around her legs, the vambraces snapping onto her forearms and so on. As they do, so does the shrivelled flesh beneath fill with renewed vigour, the parchment like skin pinking up with blood, the eyes forming in the yellowed skull, muscles filling out.

You step back, hand on your weapon, as the murals around the wall are also restored to their former glory. And now you can see in pictures the tale of the Lost Hero.

With a sigh you pretend to relent. 'All right, let's move a few hundred yards that way where the water's a bit deeper.'

After you've been coaching them for a few minutes, they start to lose interest. Little kids have the attention span of gnats. You leave them cavorting in the stream and go back to your favourite spot.

'My blue chiton!' she booms. 'How could you?' And with that, she swings a golden fist at your head. You duck under it fairly easily but it soon becomes apparent that there is nothing you can do to damage her dense golden skin. One clubbing blow from her fist could take you down in an instant, should it connect.

You realise you are now fighting for the chance to escape. Make a Strength or Grace roll at difficulty 8.

Fail goto [**633**](#_633)

#### 364

The barge slowly slides upriver, the shade propelling the _Shadow on the Water_ with surprising strength for a ghost.

On the riverbank squats a low temple of black rock. Even the pillars at the portico front are of black marble, inlaid with thin silver threads.

'What is that?' you ask.

'The Necromanteion. The oracle of the dead. This is the end of the ride, I can drop you here if you like, or at the bridge a little further upriver.'

#### 365

It is as black as Tantalus' heart. You think you spotted an oil lamp amidst the grave goods and you scrabble about in the dark for it - yes, there it is. You light it up.

In the flickering light, shadows dance across the walls like capering shades at some underworld midnight ball. You look around and are able to find out more about Pelops life from the friezes and wall paintings, and it chills you to the bone.

Tantalus had served in Olympus but he stole ambrosia and other secrets from the gods and was cast out. Bitter and resentful, he murdered his son Pelops, chopped him into bits, boiled him up and served him to the gods for dinner so that they would be defiled by it. But the gods knew it was human flesh, and refused it - all save Demeter who distractedly took a mouthful. Outraged by this foul act, the gods resurrected Pelops and he lived for a time in Olympus. But his shoulder was missing, having been eaten by Demeter so she gave him an artificial one made from ivory. Pelops lived a long and happy(ish) life as King of the Peloponnese (named for him) but his father was punished severely for his foul crimes.

Seems like Tantalus deserved his fate! You search around for an exit or a way to open the door. In the process you find a **royal diadem** and an **ivory shoulder blade**. Note them on your adventure sheet.

However, you haven't found a way to get out of here, and that you must if you are to wreak your vengeance on treacherous Tantalus. Make an Ingenuity roll at difficulty 8.

Succeed goto [**78**](#_78)

Fail goto [**441**](#_441)

#### 373

'I was a Spartan princess, married to a King of Lydia. Agamemnon slew him in battle, murdered our child. To me he said 'marriage or death' so I became his wife. Later I bore him a son, Orestes, and a daughter, Iphigenia. To win the favour of the gods in the war with Troy, Agamemnon sacrificed her. My daughter, like she was nothing more than a sheep or a goat because, in the eyes of men, we are mere chattel. But I was avenged, I slew him. And then my son, my own son, took his vengeance in turn, and cut me down, his mother! What are we women but playthings to gods and men, curse them all.'

'Agamemnon has conquered Elysium,' you say. 'Even the shades of the dead will suffer under his cruel rule if he is not driven out. What are his weaknesses, how can I defeat him?'

'A knife in the back whilst he bathed worked for me,' she says, the twitch of a smile on her phantasmal face. 'But that was then, when blood was quick, and hearts were filled with passion and rage. Now, there is only shadow and sadness. Though... perhaps his sceptre, his staff of office. Made by the gods themselves, it gave him power and authority over others. Destroy that and maybe you can destroy him.'

You nod, thank her, and turn to leave. Klytemnestra turns back to bitter contemplation of her fate. You return to the road in the Mourning Fields.

#### 382

[TICKBOX]

Put a tick in the box. If there is a tick in the box already goto [**287**](#_287) immediately. If not, read on.

You have returned to the haunted farmhouse where the ghost of a girl was trapped in the old well, murdered, tragically by her mother. But something is different. The signpost at the entrance that once said 'Execratos' now reads 'Exagorazo'. And the farm no longer seems ruined. In fact, all is at it once might have been on earth in times gone by, yet hazy and indistinct. The sky seems blue and the sun shines down on a bucolic scene but as if through a muslin veil. Chickens run in the front courtyard, chased by a dog. A woman leans out of the farm door, calling for her daughter, who runs, laughing, from the well, a pail of water in her hands. A man, one hand shielding his eyes from the sun, the other holding a bronze bladed scythe on one shoulder, walks over, smiling broadly. The harvest is ready....

It is a scene of what once might have been or even what has been after all? But then it all fades from your sight. Happy laughter is replaced with the faint sound of the wailing dead, carried to your ears by the sepulchral winds of Hades. The farmhouse is now broken and decayed, nothing more than low walls and rubble. A tattered wooden shutter bangs in the breeze.

The well is choked with mud and dust. But what is that by its edge? Something familiar - a beautifully crafted wooden box, with dryads and satyrs exquisitely carved on the lid. Inside you find three **lava gems** and a small bone flute. It too is beautifully carved with dryads. But what kind of bone is it? Your suspicions are heightened when you see it is inscribed with the word 'Koré', for that means young girl or maiden in old Greek. This flute couldn't be carved from the bones of the murdered girl, could it? On the other hand, Koré is also a name for Persephone, the Queen of the Underworld so perhaps it is a gift from her. Yes, best to keep telling yourself that. Note the **bone flute of Koré** on your Adventure Sheet.

There is nothing more for you here, so you return to the Plains of Howling Darkness.

#### 418

You stand before the Gate of Ivory, one of the entrances into Hades. #### 419

You leave the toilet through a door of black marble and step out into a kind of vestibule, similarly appointed with a black and silver colour scheme with sliver wash basins and an attendant. #### 421

You grab her by her forearms, and haul her out of the yellow waters that reek of rotten eggs. She lies gasping on the ice. She seems an adventurous sort, dressed in black leathers with a blue cap and tunic and baggy embroidered blue and gold trousers in the Scythian style, but armed only with a long dagger by her side. Her red hair and pale complexion give her a fay aspect.

'Th..th.. thank you,' she manages. You have to carry her to the lakeshore where you quickly get a campfire going and soon she is warming herself by the fire. She says her name is Orphea, a wandering adventurer. You ask what she was doing on the ice, especially as she is also still alive, like you.

'That lake... I... umm.. a family heirloom, it is somewhere in those waters.'

'What, and you swam down into it?'

'No, that's too dangerous, far too cold. I was actually fishing for it, I'd made a hole but it broke apart and I fell in.'

'Fishing for it? You really thought you'd find it with a fishing rod?'

'Well, it had a lodestone on the end, you know magnetic. For finding... metal things. But I've lost that now, fell into the water. I suppose I shall have to try something else.'

'How did you get into Hades anyway?' you ask.

'How did you?' she counters.

'Oh, that's easy. I'm the chosen one of the gods, here to save the Vulcanverse,' you answer glibly.

She stares at you for a few seconds. 'Right,' she says. 'Well, I did it the old fashioned way - I hid under a tarpaulin on Charon's barge. The old fool didn't notice a thing.'

'Hah! Must be a valued heirloom indeed to go to all that effort,' you say. She nods non-committally and changes the subject.

You share some of your rations with her, as you both warm yourselves by the fire. You swap tales of adventure and derring-do. It seems she is more the wandering minstrel type, singing for her supper and the like. Orphea gets out her flute and begins to play a soothing, relaxing tune. You can't help yourself and doze off, dreaming of the finer things in life.

goto [**139**](#_139)

'I made those,' she says, plaintively, reaching out with a ghostly hand, her voice a hoarse whisper as if she hadn't said a word in a hundred years. She looks up at you, saying, 'They made me do it, they made me!'

'The voices, the voices in my head, the Keres...' She drops her gaze back to the silver buckles. 'Oh my little one... ' she mutters.

Who knows how long she has been here, punishing herself.

#### 444

You are standing in the sacred treasure Vault of Vulcan in Hades, a place on the cusp of life and death, blessed by the God Vulcan himself. Shimmering pillars hold up the ceiling, itself a black shroud filled with glimmering stars. The floor is of black marble inlaid with intricate red mosaics that writhe and glow as if they were made of rivers of lava. Gingerly, you walk upon it, for it feels both unreal, as if you might fall through it at any moment and also dangerously hot, like walking on molten marble. Everywhere there are heaped piles of unimaginable treasures of all kinds. Coins, gems, silver, gold, armour, weapons, vases - inconceivable wealth. Yet it is all insubstantial and unreal. You cannot hold any of it, no matter how you try. Only that which you have left here yourself can you touch.

If you are alive, then you can take any items or money that you have left here previously out of the Vault and add them to your Adventure Sheet or store any items in the vault also - simply cross them off your Adventure Sheet and add them to the box below or vice versa. (Note that you cannot store the **sceptre of Agamemnon**, if you have it. It must remain on your Adventure Sheet).

[STASH]_ITEMS IN VAULT_

When you come back to life, you lose all your money and gear from your Adventure Sheet, but you can put any items and money you like into the Vault. 'Oh... I see... but how did you...'

'Don't you see? There was only one way out, only one way to break the spell - death. So I killed myself.'

'Wow...'

#### 459

It's only now you become aware of your brothers and sisters, all younger than you, huddled in silence at the back of the room.

'Give them to me, mother, and I'll fetch us something.'

'But then, how will he pay the ferryman?'

And it's with dread that you follow her eyes to the table, where the sheeted body of your father lies. Those two coins, placed on his eyes, would give his spirit the means to cross the river into the afterlife.

'Or should they go as an offering to the gods, who must look on us kindly if we are to survive?' wonders your mother. She sounds so lost and bewildered that you must surely stir and sob in your slumber.

#### 479

Elysium, the Elysian Fields, the Blessed Isle as it is also known, is a rather beautiful place. There is a sun in the sky, although how and what it is, is another question. Divine magic perhaps but you can feel its comforting warmth on you face, something you have sorely missed here in the underworld. The fields are green, there are trees and flowers (albeit black or red flowers but still). There are bucolic villas and palaces, streets are cobbled and clean, plazas have statues and pillared temples. Bandstands are dotted around, along with open air theatres. It's like the centre of a mighty city on earth, where the elite live and the triumphal arches, civic monuments and memorials are to be found. Or rather a pale shadow of that for everything is washed out and somehow insubstantial, for it may be a kind of heaven but it is a heaven of the dead.

And now it is under a kind of martial lock down. Ancient warrior shades, armed and armoured as Mycenaean soldiers of old from the Trojan wars patrol the streets and fields; the ordinary shades of Elysium remain indoors or hurry along the streets, doing the bidding of their new masters. They cower under the boot heels of Agamemnon, King of Elysium. These ancient warrior shades are not like the usual ghostly shades you find in Hades. They have a kind dark vigour about them, like night predators back on earth. You will have to avoid them wherever possible.

#### 499

If you have the codeword **_Neural_** goto [**812**](#_812) immediately. If not, read on.

The building is made of bricks and mortar with a simple portico and a frieze depicting a man at a workbench working on some kind of machine. You enter and surprisingly, as soon as you walk in, lanterns light up on the walls, as if by magic.

The chamber is rectangular, the walls lined with granite. Everywhere lie bits of bronze, iron and wood in various shapes and sizes. Cogs, tubes, tools, curious broken contraptions, clockwork devices of unknown purpose, screws, nails, wires and so on lie scattered about. Another bench is littered with the apparatus of alchemy - alembics, beakers, vials of chemicals and so on. You suspect the lights are not magical at all, but a clever device of some kind.

In one corner is a furnace, in the other some kind of brewing vat. You rummage around - everything is largely rusted, broken or of little use to you.

You also find a big bronze chest with a combination lock on it. Nearby is a book. It is full of complex drawings and indecipherable notes written in some kind of shorthand. However, there is a name that you can read on the inside cover.

It says: 'This book belongs to Daedalus, architect, inventor, artist, and father of a lost son.' A rather sad entry it seems. Underneath that entry you see these three questions:

_'The palace of Hades took seven years to build. Each year the spirits of the dead workmen were able to double its height. How many years did it take for the palace to reach half of its maximum height?'_

_'How much does an amphora of wine weigh if it weighs one pound plus half its own weight?'_

_'There are two head of sheep in a paddock. The shepherd enters the paddock and leaves with two heads. How many are left in the paddock?'_

Solving those puzzles will give you three digits that will open the combination lock of the big bronze chest. When you think you have an answer, write **499** in the Current Location box and turn to the number. If it is correct, it will say so. If it is not, come back here and try again.

#### 526

'I think I have the very thing,' says Protagoras. 'Remember Zeno of the Eleatic school?'

'Ah yes, another great philosopher, he of the dialectic and the paradox!' says Aspasia.

'Indeed, now, one of his paradoxes is this.' Protagoras stands and clears his throat. You fold your arms.

'Now imagine an arrow - shot from the bow of our adventurer here, for instance. Before it can hit its target, it must get halfway there, yes?'

'Agreed,' says Xenophanes.

'Drone on, you old fool,' says Aristippus, 'whilst I take your food from your plate!'

Protagoras frowns down at his emptying plate, but continues nevertheless.

'Before the arrow can get halfway there, it must get a quarter of the way there. Before travelling a quarter, it has to travel one eighth, before an eighth, a sixteenth, and so on.' You raise an eyebrow.

'Pah, utter sophistry!' says Cleanthes.

'Well, yes,' says Protagoras the Sophist, 'but the point is that for the arrow to reach its target it must complete what will be an infinite number of tasks, which is, of course, impossible even for one such as our hero here. Therefore all motion is an illusion!'

'Yes, yes, one of his dichotomies. We should think on it, not as a reality but as a tool of the mind. To wrestle with such logic will teach us humility, the limits of human understanding, of knowing that there are things we cannot know,' says Cleanthes.

'Hah, what a waste of time! All I need to know is that this almond tart, stolen from Protagoras' plate, tastes the sweeter for it!' says Aristippus.

'All very well, but how does this concern our adventuring hero?' says Aspasia.

'Because I have it. Here! Look, Zeno's arrow!' Protagoras draws an arrow out of his satchel. It has a white shaft, beautiful goose feather fletching, a good steel arrowhead. The words 'Zeno's arrow' have been etched into the shaft in red paint.

'I give it to you as your reward for helping us to prove that all things come to those that wait!'

goto [**19**](#_19)

'As if existence itself were entirely dependent on the memories of others.' says Sacrotes wistfully.

'Interesting proposition - do we exist if there is no one else to hear our words or see our deeds?' says Plota.

'Hmm... Finally we can ascribe a reason for your existence, Plota. You are here only to validate _my_ existence.' says Sacrotes.

'Oh, very amusing. But either way, here we both are in Hades, nothing but shadows on a wall, seen only through the flickering half-light of a dying fire, soon to fade from the pages of history forever.'

They shake their heads despairingly.

If you have **Zeno's arrow ?** [**37**](#_37)

If not, there nothing more to do here, so you return to the bar goto [**465**](#_465)

#### 540

As quiet as you can, you edge your way into the Temple of Arachne. It is long and low with four great pillars along each side, and a well-lit pathway running down the middle to the far end. The walls are hung with beautifully woven tapestries, showing the story of the contest between the goddess Athena and the weaver, Arachne. In the end, Athena, angry at Arachne's mortal hubris and jealous of her skill in weaving, curses her, turning her and all her descendants into spiders, so that they might weave forever, endlessly spinning webs. And here, in this temple in the swamps of Hades, her children have made their home. You avoid the centrally lit walkway, and make your way down one shadowed side to the altar. As you draw nearer, a terrifying sight makes you pull up short. Tending to the altar is a hideous creature - her bottom half the body of a spider, her top half similar to the torso of a human woman, but knobbed and ridged with cartilage. A spiderling, spawn of Arachne. Suddenly, she turns her head and sniffs.

Each blessing can be used to roll again when you fail an attribute check (Charm, Grace, Ingenuity or Strength) but only once in any given situation - so if you fail the re-roll, you can't spend another blessing to try again.

#### 545

In this case, discretion is the better part of valour. Whilst the kraken is engaged in a quick dinner, you hide yourself behind the altar to Hypnos.

You decide to investigate where the three recently eaten Argonauts had set up their campfire.

Someone has set up a stall or shop on the side of the road, here in the Asphodel Meadows. Well, at least it looks like a shop. There are literally no goods laid out; its shelves are bare. Behind the counter there is a shade dressed in a pale, white apron and a grey cap.

'I am Perseus,' says the shade in a chirpy but nevertheless sepulchral voice.

'No, no, not that Perseus, obviously. I was famous in my own way, though,' says the shade.

'Umm... no, not really. It was... well, it was cakes. I made honey cakes. Best baker in Boeotia, that's me.'

'So, you set up a cake stall. Here in Hades?' you ask incredulously, pointing at the endless meadows, the grey skies and the occasional passing dead soul.

'I know, I know, but I had a dream once. The Fates came to me and said "honey cakes in Tartarus are the future," or something like that. Anyway, what else could I do? This is all I know,' grates the shade in his ghostly voice.

'But in any case, there are no cakes,' you point out.

'Well, what do you expect? Where would I get the ingredients, here in hell, eh?' says the shade.

'Hah! Interesting. What do you need?' you ask, your curiosity piqued.

'Not much, just some honey and grain.'

#### 571

Thankfully, your precipitous fall starts slowing down, until you come to a full halt, hovering in the air, in total darkness. You are able to gently step down onto to a hard surface.

You are in a rough chamber, hewn out of solid rock. The floor is covered with dirt and detritus, the remains of things that have fallen into this hole over the years.

One side of the chamber has a massive set of iron doors set into it. There are no door handles but instead, in the middle, hangs a bronze visage, the face of a gorgon, all snake hair and fangs.

'Wha... what? Is someone there?' says the door-face in a surprisingly pleasant voice.

You and Deucalion stare in surprise.

'Mortals? Live mortals?' says the door-face. 'What are you doing here?'

'We're here to rescue Prometheus,' says Deucalion.

'What? Oh yes! Of course, of course you are. That's why I'm here, yes, yes, I remember now - it's been so long! Anyway, I'm supposed to set you a puzzle, if you solve it, I'll open the door for you. Now...what was it?'

You fold your arms. Deucalion taps a foot impatiently.

'Ah yes! So, at a philosophers' picnic, ten philosophers had wine, eight had cake, and six had both wine and cake. How many philosophers were there at the picnic?'

'Any idea, Deucalion?' you say.

'Me? I grew up so fast there was no time for a proper education. It's a miracle I can even speak, let alone do maths!'

It looks like you will have to give the answer. What will you say?

#### 590

You climb up the hill, hands held over your ears, to Salmoneus and his thundering gongs. It is in fact an easy task to dismantle the magic bronze gongs and soon all is mercifully quiet.

'You must be one of my worshippers! Finally you realise that I am the true god and it is time to forsake that charlatan, Zeus, am I right?'

'Well, not really...' you begin to say.

You shake your head. Clearly his endless punishment has driven him mad. Salmoneus, seeing that you are not listening to him, strides to the shoreline and begins commanding the hydras of the bayou to bow down to him, and take him to his throne on Olympus. He keeps screaming like this until suddenly a mass of tentacles writhe up out of the black waters and take him, still shouting commands, down into the depths, never to be seen again. At least his suffering is over....

Hydna refuses to take you to any of the other hills for fear of incurring the disfavour of the gods herself. #### 603

You enter the Chamber of the Furies. It is the heart of the hell within Hades itself, a huge, vast chamber of black granite, lit by ever-burning bonfires seemingly placed at random here and there in the great chamber. Everywhere, the shades of those who were oath-breakers, kin-slayers, and blasphemers in life, and those who committed unspeakable acts of evil suffer the pains of torment in all sorts of ways as decreed by the gods, the fates, the furies or their own guilt. Many are kept in cages, others in fiery pools, some in filthy waters, or narrow shafts sunk into the ground, others carry out the same tasks, over and over again. Many and varied are the torments.

High up in the rooftops, alongside those who are hung up there or left swinging from chains above, are the nests of the Furies themselves who swoop down every now and then to oversee all this agony. They are human like, but with bat like wings, heads more like wolves than humans, yellow-eyed and fanged. They carry brass-studded whips and live only to punish the wicked which they do with angry delight.

This is a truly awful place of agony, of despair and endless suffering. Do mortals really deserve this, you wonder to yourself, no matter what they did in life?

#### 621

You have solved the combination lock puzzle on the big bronze chest.

Six - if it doubles every year, half its height must be in the penultimate year.

Two - half its own weight must be equal to one, therefore the answer is two.

#### 626

'You slowly die of thirst I guess,' says the face. 'Goodbye!' With that the silver minotaur closes his eyes and appears to go back to sleep.

You and Deucalion exchange a despairing glance. But then you have an idea. 'Perhaps I can talk our way out of this,' you say. If you have the codewords **_Namesake_** or **_Nihilism_** (or both) goto [**710**](#_710) immediately. If not, read on.
