#### 678

You leave her tomb lair and find yourself in the Mourning Fields, one of the quarters of the City of the Dead. Her lair was virtually round the corner from your own house! You look around. The Mourning Fields is a place for those who cannot break the bonds of grief and sorrow, even in death. Here, the Houses of the Dead are laid out in a grid, like a cemetery in the world of the living but much of it is in a crumbling state of decay. The inhabitants do not care about their surroundings, no offerings are left at the shrines, the walkways are pitted and overgrown with the thorny, black brambles of Hades, or overshadowed by the skeletal branches of bone-white Poplar trees. Doors hang on hinges, roofs have collapsed.

Everywhere there is an all-pervading sense of sorrow and despair, and wherever you go, you cannot escape the sound of sobbing or the keening wails of grief and misery that pierce the gloom like needles of anguish. This is an awful place.

Nearby, you see moss-green hand prints on the portal of a tomb. From within, sobbing can be heard. Opposite the tomb is a crumbling building with a faded sign that says, 'The Baths of Klytemnestra.' Further on, the road descends deeper into the Fields.

'Is it true that Zeus made her love you?'

He starts to sob into his drink. 'Yes, it's true. It was a false, forced love. But I love her still, how could I not? She is the goddess of love and so I am cursed to be heartbroken forever.'

He looks up. 'Well, yes, I have this,' says Anchises, and he fetches out a small, beautifully carved wooden box, inlaid with scenes of love and marriage. He opens it. Inside is a little heart-shaped ball of light, glowing softly. 'It is the kiss of the goddess made real,' he says.

'Who knows?'

'Interesting. What do you want for it?'

#### 693

Aspasia continues. 'This merchant was from Ephesus. He trades with a merchant from Persia, who in turn trades with a merchant from Gandhara in India. That merchant trades even farther afield with the land of the Seres, at the end of the Silk road. He returned with a short piece from a philosopher of that land, translated into Greek. I believe it comes from a school called "Tao". I know little more than that, but I found the proposition intriguing and thought it would entertain you.'

Aspasia gets out a scroll and reads from it.

'I, Chuang Chou, dreamt I was a butterfly, fluttering here and there, to all intents and purposes a butterfly. I was conscious only of my happiness as a butterfly, unaware that I was Chou but then I woke up to find myself back to be being Chuang Chou. The conundrum is this: I do not know whether I am a man dreaming I was a butterfly, or whether I am now a butterfly, dreaming I am a man....'

'Note, Cleanthes, that the butterfly was happy, and thus enjoying life,' says Aristippus.

'What? How can a butterfly be happy, every moment must be lived in fear of the lick of the lizard or the tongue of the toad, to be snapped up and eaten in a moment. No, the life of a butterfly is to be endured, not enjoyed!' replies Cleanthes.

'I don't think that's really the point though is it? Isn't it more about how we can reliably tell what is real, and what isn't? To consider our own senses, and how we can be certain that what we know is real?' says Xenophanes.

'Indeed, an interesting conundrum. I propose we consider this matter. We'll call it the dream of the butterfly. Let us think on it, and each will then provide a theory on the problem.' says Protagoras.

'I wonder what butterfly tastes like to the toad and the lizard,' muses Aristippus.

'Personally,' says Protagoras, I am fairly certain that Cleanthes is in fact an insect, dreaming that he is a man, and I aim to prove it, or at least to propose a theorem to that effect that cannot be disproved!'

They continue in this vein. You take your leave or you will be stuck listening to their musings for all eternity.

Aspasia says to you as you go, 'Please come back to us, adventurer - perhaps you can bring us proof one way or the other on Zeno's arrow and the dream of the butterfly, just like you did with the proposition that all things come to those that wait. Good luck, and thank you!'

#### 710

You recall the philosophers' picnic you helped create by bringing them a hamper from Arcadia and the journeys and adventures it led you to. But most importantly, you remember all the (irritating) rhetoric and absurd logic of it all.

You begin a back and forth discussion with Deucalion, remembering some of the tactics Protagoras the Sophist used to bend the logic around questions such as 'all things come to those who wait' or whether existence is but a dream, and so on. After a short while, the minotaur pipes up.

'Please, I'm trying to sleep!'

You ignore him and carry on the discussion, following a particularly circular argument about the nature of perception and the point of existence itself. For instance, is the life of a bee as valuable as the life of a philosopher? And is that as valuable as the life of a queen or for that matter, a semi-sentient being whose only function is to set puzzles for passing humans?

'By the gods, are you mad!' cries the minotaur face in increasing agitation.

Then you introduce the concept of Zeno's arrow, and how therefore all motion is an illusion.

'Arg! I can't stand to hear another word of your so called logic! My head is going to explode,' wails the minotaur.

'Well now,' you say slyly, 'if you were to open the door and let us through, we would bother you no more.'

'I...but... I'm not supposed...'

You begin to discuss whether or not man is a dream of the butterfly.

'All right, all right! I'll let you through, just never bother me again!' With that, the great iron doors swing open, and you both step through. Deucalion claps you on the shoulder with a grin. 'Well done,' he says.

#### 715

#### 721

Hydna drops you at the shoreline of Sisyphus's hill. As you arrive, the man-sized rock rolls down the hill to the bottom. Sisyphus comes down the hill, looking bemused.

'Where... what...' he mutters. And then he looks around more alert. 'Could it be? he says, 'Can it be true? It is the rock that compels me, without it... yes, yes, I am free!' He hugs you ecstatically. 'Free at last!'

He may be free, but you have indeed offended the gods. Lose all blessings, if you have any. On the other hand, you have won the gratitude of Sisyphus.

You take him on the barge with Hydna back to land. He thanks you profusely and then leaves, saying that he has to 'get his head straight' after so such endless toil but that if you should ever need him, as long as it is not to help the Olympian gods whom he can never forgive for what they have done to him, he will come to your aid.

'And remember, I am cunning! More cunning than the gods, my ingenuity knows no bounds!' are Sisyphus's parting words. Gain the title **Sisyphean Taskmaster**.

You consider the other two, but Hydna refuses to take you for fear of bringing the disfavour of the gods down on her own head too. You could swim to them but you know the bayou is infested with tentacled horrors and how would you get out after without Hydna's barge? It is time to say your farewells to Hydna and leave the Black Bayou.

#### 730

You descend further into the depths of the underworld. The stairwell opens out into a vast, underground cavern filled with a great roaring sound. Up above, the roof disappears into darkness, but water drips down from stalactites that point down out of the dark. The cavern is lit with a flickering orange-red glow, as if from some great fire, but you cannot see the source of the flames. You walk on through a forest of man-high stalagmites, until you come to the source of the great roaring sound and of the flames, at the centre of the cavern - the headwaters of all the rivers of Hades.

A large black mound of solid rock pushes up from the ground like a small volcano, and out of its top spout five great streams than run down its sides and off to the nooks and corners of this cavern, where they disappear underground.

One of them is the Phlegethon, the river of fire, and its burning flames are the source of the light. The others are the Acheron, the river of woe, the Styx, the river of hate, the Lethe, the river of forgetfulness, and the Cocytus, the icy river of wailing, though the last is more of an ice floe than a river like frozen sludge. As the lumps of ice grind up against each other, they give off an eerie wailing sound...

The cavern is vast and empty save for the keening wail of the ice and the eldritch light of the flames of hell. You are alone at its centre...

You feel abandoned, and without hope, here at the heart of the underworld. You pull yourself together - time to get on with something that will clear your head.

If you have **Lethe water** you may refill your vial up to 3 drafts (add **x3** next to it). If it is already full than you cannot fill it any more. It seems only this magic vial can hold the waters of the Lethe.

Agamemnon holds his sceptre in one hand, more like a staff of silver and gold than a sceptre. It was built by Vulcan himself, Zeus carried it, it was handed down to Pelops, son of Tantalus, and eventually to Pelops' grandson, Agamemnon, who used it to command the armies of the Archaeans at Troy. The key of copper and jade fits perfectly into the lock. You turn it with ease. There's a whirring, clanking sound and then slowly, ever so slowly the ancient door creaks open. You step through, into what would have been an amazing vista of a formal, classical garden There are fountains, statues, gazebos and arbours. Pomegranate trees line marble walkways. Plant boxes line every pathway. Bone-white poplars are mixed in with black-barked trees to make contrasting patterns.

Or at least they did.... Now everything has fallen into decay and ruin. The fountains bubble no more, the statues are stained with filth. Everywhere, blood-red weeds, copper-coloured crimson-tipped thorns and black brambles choke the life out of everything.

As you draw near, you see four male shades and one female shade who seems to be in charge, seated around a brightly coloured rug that has been laid out over the grave-cold earth of Hades. The woman is seated on a low chair, the others on the rug. Cups, jugs and plates have been laid out but you cannot see any food or drink at all. They all appear to be silently staring at the rug, as if expecting something to happen. You walk up to them and ask. 'What have we here?'

'Welcome,' says the woman, her eyes keen and bright, even in death, and dressed in a relatively clean and fresh funeral shroud, 'to the philosophers' picnic!'

'But there is nothing to eat or drink.' you say.

'Ah, indeed, and there's the rub,' says another.

'You see, Protagoras here,' says the woman, pointing at the shade sitting next to her, 'of the Sophist school, has proposed that all things come to those who wait.'

You cross your arms.

Another shade, in a cap with a staff and voluminous robes and looking very, very old, pipes up, 'And I, Xenophanes, proposed that we test this hypothesis by waiting.'

You scratch your head.

'And I,' says another with a long beard and a scroll under his arm, 'Cleanthes the Stoic agree - because it is the waiting that is the thing from which we can learn, not the outcome. After all, whether the proposition be true or not is of no import!'

You run your hand through your hair and sigh.

'And I, Aristippus the Cyrenaic couldn't care less about any of that - all I want is to stuff my face with food and drink!' says a fat, bald philosopher. 'For what else matters?'

'And I, Aspasia, chair of this philosophers' salon in Hades have allowed it, on the basis that we've got nothing else to do and plenty of time!'

A shade dressed in a painter's smock sits before a blank canvas, gazing at the fiery Phlegethon river, whose flaming waters fill the grey skies of Hades with a reddish, luminescent glow. The river banks are too hot and charred for anything to grow there, but further away, a few white poplars grow, their bone-white bark seeming to sparkle with crimson light in the glow from the river of fire. Off to the right, where the Styx meets the Phlegethon, steam roils upward, in ruby-edged clouds of vapour.

The view is apocalyptic, the light and the colours utterly unique. Nowhere else in the world, or even all of the worlds that are, have been or shall be, would you find such a view.

As you approach, the painter, staring at the sky, finally notices you.

'Oh, hello,' he says, looking you up and own. 'Well, well, you appear to be actually alive!'

'I am,' you say. 'And who are you?'

'I am Apelles!' he says proudly, thrusting out his spectral chest.

'Who?' you say.

'What do you mean who? You've never heard of Apelles, one of the greatest painters ever to walk the green grass of Arcadia!'

'Umm... no.'

'Bah, ignorant barbarian!' mutters Apelles. 'Not that it matters really,' he adds. He gestures to his paint palette. 'I haven't got any paint. Haven't had any for ages, can't get any down here either, so I haven't laid a single brushstroke to canvas in years!'

'It's a spectacular view,' you comment.

'Indeed! If only I had some paint, it could be one of my greatest paintings ever! You haven't got any, have you?' says Apelles.

#### 803

The mural shows the Trojan war in full flow, with Achilles doing battle against the Trojans. But by his side is a woman warrior, an Amazon princess it seems, who left her tribe for love and married a Greek king, and so ending up fighting for the Achaeans. Her deeds are seemingly greater and more heroic than those of Achilles for she battles the gods themselves. Apollo shoots an arrow into her eye, but she plucks it out and fights on, throwing the god down and humiliating him. Later, she shatters Poseidon's trident with her shield and even wounds the goddess Aphrodite with her spear. Even though the Lost Hero was doing Zeus' will in fighting for the Greeks, this was too great an offence against the gods, and they punished her for her hubris - by erasing the memory of her great deeds, her prowess in battle and all of her life's achievements from history. She became the Lost Hero, never to be remembered, never to be honoured, never to be recognized, no songs sung of her deeds, no epic poems written about her. Homer himself never knew that she'd even been born. She would sleep in anonymity, lost and forgotten, until the day would come when they would need her again...

The Lost Hero rises up from her dais, taking up the ancient shield, now looking bright and new, with the face of a one eyed cyclops painted on it and a long spear, its head still stained with blood, presumably Aphrodite's. Even though she has been resurrected somehow, you notice she is still blind in one eye from the arrow of Apollo. Such wounds never heal, you suppose.

You step back, unsure what is going to happen next. Can even you fight such a warrior?

But then she speaks. 'I am Lethia, the Lost Hero.' Her voice is strong, imperious and imposing. But even though she said her name only seconds ago, in a voice that draws your attention strongly, you find yourself struggling to remember it.

'You have brought my panoply of armour to me, and thus, as the gods decreed, do I live again. The gods granted me this boon, my one chance to redeem my honour and lift this curse, by serving you.'

You stand somewhat flabbergasted, dumbstruck. Her antique armour, where once it was rusty and begrimed is now brightly burnished, inlaid with blue and red. Her helmet has a magnificent red and blue headdress, the bronze metal encasing her head, but also a section of it coming down over her lost eye. She is tall and powerfully build as well as lithe and dextrous. She is the closest thing you could imagine to a goddess of war without actually being divine.

And then she hands you the Horn of the Lost Hero.

'When the time comes, blow this and I will come to fight by your side,' she says. 'Farewell, until we meet again.'

Suddenly you find yourself outside the tomb, the door sealed up once more. Cross off the **Panoply of the Lost Hero**, gain the codeword **_Nanny_** and goto [**749**](#_749)

#### 809

The scroll explains that Typhon was a terrible dragon. He was said to have a hundred heads with eyes that could shoot fireballs, and he fought a mighty battle with Zeus, king of the gods, for control of the world. Typhon hurled mountains at Zeus, who threw thunderbolts back at him. After a titanic battle, Zeus was triumphant, blowing bits and pieces off of Typhon along the way that fell across all the lands, before hurling the dread monster king back down into the depths of Tartarus.

To cast the spell, you need the binding spell, written on a clay tablet, now shattered into three fragments, and one of Typhon's eyeballs. A clay spell fragment lies propped up against the shrine. All you need are the other two fragments and, well, Typhon's eyeball. Then you can summon the power of Typhon. Shouldn't be too hard, right?

Record that you have the first fragment of the binding spell on your Adventure Sheet - in your notes, not as an item, as you cannot lose it. This fragment reads:

_'O Gaia, the Earth, Mother of Gods and Monsters, hear my words! Grant me charge of your son, the Terrible One, he whose breath is fire!_

_'O primordial Tartarus, Lord of the Abyss, Father of the Fearful Dragon, I invoke thee! Grant me the rod over your son, he whose voice is sound unspeakable!'_

Once you have found all three spell fragments and the **Eye of Typhon**, get the codeword **_Neophyte_**. You will have to return here to cast the spell as it will only work at this shrine. Good luck!

You hear the sound of iron-shod hooves thundering across the paved stones of the Sacred Way behind you. Turning, you see a massively muscled horse, as black as night, cantering towards you. It is terrifying enough but nothing compared to the rider. He is dressed all in black, wielding a single-bladed scythe-like axe in one hand, and a shield emblazoned with a stylized black vulture in the other. Red eyes glare at you from under a horned helm of unusual design.

Down out of the sky, black-feathered vultures swoop, circling the rider.

'I am Death Dealer, the champion of Thanatos,' says the black rider, in a steely, doom-laden voice that also sounds empty of feeling, pitiless like a cold north wind. 'The sleeping gods may dream up a decree that you shall not die, but you are a mortal and such things are an abomination in the eyes of the god of death, and cannot be endured.'

'Does not Thanatos sleep then?' you ask.

'Death does not sleep,' replies the Death Dealer.

'But surely, if you kill me, I will just come back to life again, so why bother?' you say.

'I'm not going to kill you. No, I shall drag you in chains before my master. He will be the final arbiter, for even the gods cannot save you from Thanatos, he whose touch is death.'

The Death Dealer dismounts, hefting his axe.
